<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_and_related_materials">Definitions and related materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions and related materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Steel_grades" title="Steel grades">Steel grades</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blacksmithing_at_the_2015_Fort_Ross_Festival_-_Fort_Ross_State_Historic_Park_-_Jenner,_California_-_Sarah_Stierch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="3943" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Blacksmithing_at_the_2015_Fort_Ross_Festival_-_Fort_Ross_State_Historic_Park_-_Jenner%2C_California_-_Sarah_Stierch.jpg/240px-Blacksmithing_at_the_2015_Fort_Ross_Festival_-_Fort_Ross_State_Historic_Park_-_Jenner%2C_California_-_Sarah_Stierch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Blacksmithing_at_the_2015_Fort_Ross_Festival_-_Fort_Ross_State_Historic_Park_-_Jenner%2C_California_-_Sarah_Stierch.jpg/360px-Blacksmithing_at_the_2015_Fort_Ross_Festival_-_Fort_Ross_State_Historic_Park_-_Jenner%2C_California_-_Sarah_Stierch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Blacksmithing_at_the_2015_Fort_Ross_Festival_-_Fort_Ross_State_Historic_Park_-_Jenner%2C_California_-_Sarah_Stierch.jpg/480px-Blacksmithing_at_the_2015_Fort_Ross_Festival_-_Fort_Ross_State_Historic_Park_-_Jenner%2C_California_-_Sarah_Stierch.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blacksmithing_at_the_2015_Fort_Ross_Festival_-_Fort_Ross_State_Historic_Park_-_Jenner,_California_-_Sarah_Stierch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incandescent" title="Incandescent">Incandescent</a> steel workpiece in this depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Blacksmith" title="Blacksmith">blacksmith</a>'s art</div></div></div>
<p>The noun <i>steel</i> originates from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a> adjective <i>stahliją</i> or <i>stakhlijan</i> 'made of steel', which is related to <i>stahlaz</i> or <i>stahliją</i> 'standing firm'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The carbon content of steel is between 0.002% and 2.14% by weight for plain carbon steel (<a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>-<a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a>). Too little carbon content leaves (pure) iron quite soft, ductile, and weak. Carbon contents higher than those of steel make a brittle alloy commonly called <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alloy_steel" title="Alloy steel">Alloy steel</a> is steel to which other alloying elements have been intentionally added to modify the characteristics of steel. Common alloying elements include: <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">niobium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EM2_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-EM2-5">[5]</a></sup> Additional elements, most frequently considered undesirable, are also important in steel: <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, and traces of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>.
</p><p>Plain carbon-iron alloys with a higher than 2.1% carbon content are known as <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a>. With modern <a href="/wiki/Steelmaking" title="Steelmaking">steelmaking</a> techniques such as powder metal forming, it is possible to make very high-carbon (and other alloy material) steels, but such are not common. Cast iron is not malleable even when hot, but it can be formed by <a href="/wiki/Casting" title="Casting">casting</a> as it has a lower <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a> than steel and good <a href="/wiki/Castability" title="Castability">castability</a> properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EM2_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-EM2-5">[5]</a></sup> Certain compositions of cast iron, while retaining the economies of melting and casting, can be heat treated after casting to make <a href="/wiki/Malleable_iron" title="Malleable iron">malleable iron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ductile_iron" title="Ductile iron">ductile iron</a> objects. Steel is distinguishable from <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> (now largely obsolete), which may contain a small amount of carbon but large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Slag" title="Slag">slag</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Material_properties">Material properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Material properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iron_carbon_phase_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Iron_carbon_phase_diagram.svg/240px-Iron_carbon_phase_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Iron_carbon_phase_diagram.svg/360px-Iron_carbon_phase_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Iron_carbon_phase_diagram.svg/480px-Iron_carbon_phase_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iron_carbon_phase_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iron-carbon <a href="/wiki/Phase_diagram" title="Phase diagram">phase diagram</a>, showing the conditions necessary to form different phases</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_production">Origins and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Iron is commonly found in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> in the form of an <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a>, usually an iron oxide, such as <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a>. Iron is extracted from <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a> by removing the oxygen through its combination with a preferred chemical partner such as carbon which is then lost to the atmosphere as carbon dioxide. This process, known as <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a>, was first applied to metals with lower <a href="/wiki/Melting" title="Melting">melting</a> points, such as <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, which melts at about 250 °C (482 °F), and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, which melts at about 1,100 °C (2,010 °F), and the combination, bronze, which has a melting point lower than 1,083 °C (1,981 °F). In comparison, cast iron melts at about 1,375 °C (2,507 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smelting_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smelting-6">[6]</a></sup> Small quantities of iron were smelted in ancient times, in the solid-state, by heating the ore in a <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> fire and then <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a> the clumps together with a hammer and in the process squeezing out the impurities. With care, the carbon content could be controlled by moving it around in the fire. Unlike copper and tin, liquid or solid iron dissolves carbon quite readily.
</p><p>All of these temperatures could be reached with ancient methods used since the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>. Since the oxidation rate of iron increases rapidly beyond 800 °C (1,470 °F), it is important that smelting take place in a low-oxygen environment. Smelting, using carbon to reduce iron oxides, results in an alloy (<a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a>) that retains too much carbon to be called steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smelting_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smelting-6">[6]</a></sup> The excess carbon and other impurities are removed in a subsequent step.
</p><p>Other materials are often added to the iron/carbon mixture to produce steel with the desired properties. <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">Nickel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> in steel add to its tensile strength and make the <a href="/wiki/Austenite" title="Austenite">austenite</a> form of the iron-carbon solution more stable, <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a> increases hardness and melting temperature, and <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a> also increases hardness while making it less prone to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_fatigue" title="Metal fatigue">metal fatigue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-materialsengineer_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-materialsengineer-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>To inhibit corrosion, at least 11% chromium can be added to steel so that a hard <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">oxide</a> forms on the metal surface; this is known as <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a>. Tungsten slows the formation of <a href="/wiki/Cementite" title="Cementite">cementite</a>, keeping carbon in the iron matrix and allowing <a href="/wiki/Martensite" title="Martensite">martensite</a> to preferentially form at slower quench rates, resulting in <a href="/wiki/High-speed_steel" title="High-speed steel">high-speed steel</a>. The addition of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> decrease grain size, thereby making the steel easier to <a href="/wiki/Lathe" title="Lathe">turn</a>, but also more brittle and prone to corrosion. Such alloys are nevertheless frequently used for components such as nuts, bolts, and washers in applications where toughness and corrosion resistance are not paramount. For the most part, however, <a href="/wiki/Block_(periodic_table)" title="Block (periodic table)">p-block</a> elements such as sulfur, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, and lead are considered contaminants that make steel more brittle and are therefore removed from the steel melt during processing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-materialsengineer_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-materialsengineer-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of steel varies based on the alloying constituents but usually ranges between 7,750 and 8,050 kg/m<sup>3</sup> (484 and 503 lb/cu ft), or 7.75 and 8.05 g/cm<sup>3</sup> (4.48 and 4.65 oz/cu in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even in a narrow range of concentrations of mixtures of carbon and iron that make steel, several different metallurgical structures, with very different properties can form. Understanding such properties is essential to making quality steel. At <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a>, the most stable form of pure iron is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body-centred_cubic" title="Body-centred cubic">body-centred cubic</a> (BCC) structure called alpha iron or α-iron. It is a fairly soft metal that can dissolve only a small concentration of carbon, no more than 0.005% at 0 °C (32 °F) and 0.021 wt% at 723 °C (1,333 °F). The inclusion of carbon in alpha iron is called <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_iron" title="Allotropes of iron">ferrite</a>. At 910 °C, pure iron transforms into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Face-centred_cubic" title="Face-centred cubic">face-centred cubic</a> (FCC) structure, called gamma iron or γ-iron. The inclusion of carbon in gamma iron is called austenite. The more open FCC structure of austenite can dissolve considerably more carbon, as much as 2.1%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> (38 times that of ferrite) carbon at 1,148 °C (2,098 °F), which reflects the upper carbon content of steel, beyond which is cast iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> When carbon moves out of solution with iron, it forms a very hard, but brittle material called cementite (Fe<sub>3</sub>C).
</p><p>When steels with exactly 0.8% carbon (known as a eutectoid steel), are cooled, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austenitic" title="Austenitic">austenitic</a> phase (FCC) of the mixture attempts to revert to the ferrite phase (BCC). The carbon no longer fits within the FCC austenite structure, resulting in an excess of carbon. One way for carbon to leave the austenite is for it to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitate" title="Precipitate">precipitate</a> out of solution as <a href="/wiki/Cementite" title="Cementite">cementite</a>, leaving behind a surrounding phase of BCC iron called ferrite with a small percentage of carbon in solution. The two, ferrite and cementite, precipitate simultaneously producing a layered structure called <a href="/wiki/Pearlite" title="Pearlite">pearlite</a>, named for its resemblance to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mother_of_pearl" title="Mother of pearl">mother of pearl</a>. In a hypereutectoid composition (greater than 0.8% carbon), the carbon will first precipitate out as large inclusions of cementite at the austenite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_boundaries" title="Grain boundaries">grain boundaries</a> until the percentage of carbon in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_(metal)" title="Grain (metal)">grains</a> has decreased to the eutectoid composition (0.8% carbon), at which point the pearlite structure forms. For steels that have less than 0.8% carbon (hypoeutectoid), ferrite will first form within the grains until the remaining composition rises to 0.8% of carbon, at which point the pearlite structure will form. No large inclusions of cementite will form at the boundaries in hypoeuctoid steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The above assumes that the cooling process is very slow, allowing enough time for the carbon to migrate.
</p><p>As the rate of cooling is increased the carbon will have less time to migrate to form carbide at the grain boundaries but will have increasingly large amounts of pearlite of a finer and finer structure within the grains; hence the carbide is more widely dispersed and acts to prevent slip of defects within those grains, resulting in hardening of the steel. At the very high cooling rates produced by quenching, the carbon has no time to migrate but is locked within the face-centred austenite and forms <a href="/wiki/Martensite" title="Martensite">martensite</a>. Martensite is a highly strained and stressed, supersaturated form of carbon and iron and is exceedingly hard but brittle. Depending on the carbon content, the martensitic phase takes different forms. Below 0.2% carbon, it takes on a ferrite BCC crystal form, but at higher carbon content it takes a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body-centred_tetragonal" title="Body-centred tetragonal">body-centred tetragonal</a> (BCT) structure. There is no thermal <a href="/wiki/Activation_energy" title="Activation energy">activation energy</a> for the transformation from austenite to martensite.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> There is no compositional change so the atoms generally retain their same neighbors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith&amp;hashemi_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith&amp;hashemi-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Martensite has a lower density (it expands during the cooling) than does austenite, so that the transformation between them results in a change of volume. In this case, expansion occurs. Internal stresses from this expansion generally take the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_compression" title="Physical compression">compression</a> on the crystals of martensite and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tension_(mechanics)" title="Tension (mechanics)">tension</a> on the remaining ferrite, with a fair amount of <a href="/wiki/Shear_stress" title="Shear stress">shear</a> on both constituents. If quenching is done improperly, the internal stresses can cause a part to shatter as it cools. At the very least, they cause internal <a href="/wiki/Work_hardening" title="Work hardening">work hardening</a> and other microscopic imperfections. It is common for quench cracks to form when steel is water quenched, although they may not always be visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heat_treatment">Heat treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Heat treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Steel_Fe-C_phase_diagram-en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2350" data-file-width="1995" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Steel_Fe-C_phase_diagram-en.png/240px-Steel_Fe-C_phase_diagram-en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Steel_Fe-C_phase_diagram-en.png/360px-Steel_Fe-C_phase_diagram-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Steel_Fe-C_phase_diagram-en.png/480px-Steel_Fe-C_phase_diagram-en.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steel_Fe-C_phase_diagram-en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fe-C phase diagram for carbon steels; showing the A<sub>0</sub>, A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub> and A<sub>3</sub> critical temperatures for heat treatments.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heat_treating" title="Heat treating">Heat treating</a></div>
<p>There are many types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_treatment" title="Heat treatment">heat treating</a> processes available to steel. The most common are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy)" title="Annealing (metallurgy)">annealing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quenching" title="Quenching">quenching</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tempering_(metallurgy)" title="Tempering (metallurgy)">tempering</a>.
</p><p>Annealing is the process of heating the steel to a sufficiently high temperature to relieve local internal stresses. It does not create a general softening of the product but only locally relieves strains and stresses locked up within the material. Annealing goes through three phases: <a href="/wiki/Recovery_(metallurgy)" title="Recovery (metallurgy)">recovery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recrystallization_(metallurgy)" title="Recrystallization (metallurgy)">recrystallization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grain_growth" title="Grain growth">grain growth</a>. The temperature required to anneal a particular steel depends on the type of annealing to be achieved and the alloying constituents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Quenching involves heating the steel to create the austenite phase then quenching it in water or <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oil</a>. This rapid cooling results in a hard but brittle martensitic structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith&amp;hashemi_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-smith&amp;hashemi-12">[12]</a></sup> The steel is then tempered, which is just a specialized type of annealing, to reduce brittleness. In this application the annealing (tempering) process transforms some of the martensite into cementite, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spheroidite" title="Spheroidite">spheroidite</a> and hence it reduces the internal stresses and defects. The result is a more ductile and fracture-resistant steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Steelmaking" title="Steelmaking">Steelmaking</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_steel_production" title="List of countries by steel production">List of countries by steel production</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg/220px-LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg/330px-LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg/440px-LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">Iron ore</a> pellets for the production of steel</div></div></div>
<p>When iron is <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelted</a> from its ore, it contains more carbon than is desirable. To become steel, it must be reprocessed to reduce the carbon to the correct amount, at which point other elements can be added. In the past, steel facilities would <a href="/wiki/Casting_(metalworking)" title="Casting (metalworking)">cast</a> the raw steel product into <a href="/wiki/Ingot" title="Ingot">ingots</a> which would be stored until use in further refinement processes that resulted in the finished product. In modern facilities, the initial product is close to the final composition and is <a href="/wiki/Continuous_casting" title="Continuous casting">continuously cast</a> into long slabs, cut and shaped into bars and extrusions and heat treated to produce a final product. Today, approximately 96% of steel is continuously cast, while only 4% is produced as ingots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ingots are then heated in a soaking pit and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_rolling" title="Hot rolling">hot rolled</a> into slabs, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Billet_(semi-finished_product)#Billet" title="Billet (semi-finished product)">billets</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Billet_(semi-finished_product)#Bloom" title="Billet (semi-finished product)">blooms</a>. Slabs are hot or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold_rolling" title="Cold rolling">cold rolled</a> into <a href="/wiki/Sheet_metal" title="Sheet metal">sheet metal</a> or plates. Billets are hot or cold rolled into bars, rods, and wire. Blooms are hot or cold rolled into <a href="/wiki/Structural_steel" title="Structural steel">structural steel</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/I-beam" title="I-beam">I-beams</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_tracks" title="Rail tracks">rails</a>. In modern steel mills these processes often occur in one <a href="/wiki/Assembly_line" title="Assembly line">assembly line</a>, with ore coming in and finished steel products coming out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Sometimes after a steel's final rolling, it is heat treated for strength; however, this is relatively rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_ferrous_metallurgy" title="History of ferrous metallurgy">History of ferrous metallurgy</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_steel_industry_(1850%E2%80%931970)" title="History of the steel industry (1850–1970)">History of the steel industry (1850–1970)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bas_fourneau.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1156" data-file-width="678" decoding="async" height="409" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Bas_fourneau.png/240px-Bas_fourneau.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Bas_fourneau.png/360px-Bas_fourneau.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Bas_fourneau.png/480px-Bas_fourneau.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bas_fourneau.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bloomery" title="Bloomery">Bloomery</a> smelting during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient">Ancient</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ancient">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Steel was known in antiquity and was produced in <a href="/wiki/Bloomery" title="Bloomery">bloomeries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crucible" title="Crucible">crucibles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson199420_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson199420-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-materials.iisc.ernet.in_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-materials.iisc.ernet.in-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest known production of steel is seen in pieces of ironware excavated from an <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_site" title="Archaeological site">archaeological site</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kaman-Kaleh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="Kaman-Kalehöyük">Kaman-Kalehöyük</a>) and are nearly 4,000 years old, dating from 1800 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a> identifies steel weapons such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Falcata" title="Falcata">falcata</a></i> in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Noric_steel" title="Noric steel">Noric steel</a> was used by the <a href="/wiki/Military_of_ancient_Rome" title="Military of ancient Rome">Roman military</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The reputation of <i>Seric iron</i> of India (wootz steel) grew considerably in the rest of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-materials.iisc.ernet.in_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-materials.iisc.ernet.in-20">[20]</a></sup> Metal production sites in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> employed wind furnaces driven by the monsoon winds, capable of producing high-carbon steel. Large-scale <a href="/wiki/Wootz_steel" title="Wootz steel">Wootz steel</a> production in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> using crucibles occurred by the sixth century BC, the pioneering precursor to modern steel production and metallurgy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson199420_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson199420-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-materials.iisc.ernet.in_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-materials.iisc.ernet.in-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_China#Ancient_China" title="History of China">Chinese</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> (403–221 BC) had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quench" title="Quench">quench-hardened</a> steel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> while Chinese of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> (202 BC—AD 220) created steel by melting together wrought iron with cast iron, thus producing a carbon-intermediate steel by the 1st century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_4_part_3_563g_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_4_part_3_563g-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gernet_69_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-gernet_69-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is evidence that <a href="/wiki/Carbon_steel" title="Carbon steel">carbon steel</a> was made in Western <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> by the ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Haya_people" title="Haya people">Haya people</a> as early as 2,000 years ago by a complex process of "pre-heating" allowing temperatures inside a furnace to reach 1300 to 1400 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchmidtCS_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchmidtCS-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wootz_and_Damascus">Wootz and Damascus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wootz and Damascus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wootz_steel" title="Wootz steel">Wootz steel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damascus_steel" title="Damascus steel">Damascus steel</a></div>
<p>Evidence of the earliest production of high carbon steel in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> are found in <a href="/wiki/Kodumanal" title="Kodumanal">Kodumanal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Golconda" title="Golconda">Golconda</a> area in <a href="/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh" title="Andhra Pradesh">Andhra Pradesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>, and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samanalawewa" title="Samanalawewa">Samanalawewa</a>, Dehigaha Alakanda, areas of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> This came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Wootz_steel" title="Wootz steel">Wootz steel</a>, produced in South India by about the sixth century BC and exported globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SR_IISc_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-SR_IISc-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ann_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-ann-35">[35]</a></sup> The steel technology existed prior to 326 BC in the region as they are mentioned in literature of <a href="/wiki/Sangam_literature" title="Sangam literature">Sangam Tamil</a>, Arabic, and Latin as the finest steel in the world exported to the Romans, Egyptian, Chinese and Arab worlds at that time – what they called <i>Seric Iron</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tissamaharama_Tamil_Brahmi_inscription" title="Tissamaharama Tamil Brahmi inscription">200 BC Tamil trade guild in Tissamaharama</a>, in the South East of Sri Lanka, brought with them some of the oldest iron and steel artifacts and production processes to the island from the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahathevan_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahathevan-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ragupathy_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ragupathy-39">[39]</a></sup> The Chinese and locals in <a href="/wiki/Anuradhapura" title="Anuradhapura">Anuradhapura</a>, Sri Lanka had also adopted the production methods of creating Wootz steel from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chera_Dynasty" title="Chera Dynasty">Chera Dynasty</a> Tamils of South India by the 5th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_4_part_1_282_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_4_part_1_282-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ancient_and_Mediaeval_India._Vol.2_by_Charlotte_Speir_Manning_p.365_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ancient_and_Mediaeval_India._Vol.2_by_Charlotte_Speir_Manning_p.365-41">[41]</a></sup> In Sri Lanka, this early steel-making method employed a unique wind furnace, driven by the monsoon winds, capable of producing high-carbon steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Juleff1_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Juleff1-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_Henery_Coghlan_1977_pp_99-100_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_Henery_Coghlan_1977_pp_99-100-43">[43]</a></sup> Since the technology was acquired from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamilians" title="Tamilians">Tamilians</a> from South India,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the origin of steel technology in India can be conservatively estimated at 400–500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SR_IISc_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-SR_IISc-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_Henery_Coghlan_1977_pp_99-100_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_Henery_Coghlan_1977_pp_99-100-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The manufacture of what came to be called Wootz, or <a href="/wiki/Damascus_steel" title="Damascus steel">Damascus steel</a>, famous for its durability and ability to hold an edge, may have been taken by the Arabs from Persia, who took it from India. It was originally created from several different materials including various <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">trace elements</a>, apparently ultimately from the writings of <a href="/wiki/Zosimos_of_Panopolis" title="Zosimos of Panopolis">Zosimos of Panopolis</a>. In 327 BC, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> was rewarded by the defeated King <a href="/wiki/Porus" title="Porus">Porus</a>, not with gold or silver but with 30 pounds of steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> A recent study has speculated that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotubes" title="Carbon nanotubes">carbon nanotubes</a> were included in its structure, which might explain some of its legendary qualities, though, given the technology of that time, such qualities were produced by chance rather than by design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Natural wind was used where the soil containing iron was heated by the use of wood. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Sinhalese" title="Ancient Sinhalese">ancient Sinhalese</a> managed to extract a ton of steel for every 2 tons of soil,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Juleff1_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Juleff1-42">[42]</a></sup> a remarkable feat at the time. One such furnace was found in Samanalawewa and archaeologists were able to produce steel as the ancients did.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Juleff1_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Juleff1-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Crucible_steel" title="Crucible steel">Crucible steel</a>, formed by slowly heating and cooling pure iron and carbon (typically in the form of charcoal) in a crucible, was produced in <a href="/wiki/Merv" title="Merv">Merv</a> by the 9th to 10th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ann_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-ann-35">[35]</a></sup> In the 11th century, there is evidence of the production of steel in <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song China</a> using two techniques: a "berganesque" method that produced inferior, inhomogeneous steel, and a precursor to the modern <a href="/wiki/Bessemer_process" title="Bessemer process">Bessemer process</a> that used partial <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_economy" title="Low-carbon economy">decarbonization</a> via repeated forging under a <a href="/wiki/Cold_blast" title="Cold blast">cold blast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bessemer_Converter_Sheffield.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="456" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Bessemer_Converter_Sheffield.jpg/170px-Bessemer_Converter_Sheffield.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Bessemer_Converter_Sheffield.jpg/255px-Bessemer_Converter_Sheffield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/Bessemer_Converter_Sheffield.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bessemer_Converter_Sheffield.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Bessemer_process" title="Bessemer process">Bessemer converter</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a>, England</div></div></div>
<p>Since the 17th century, the first step in European steel production has been the smelting of iron ore into <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tyle_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-tyle-48">[48]</a></sup> Originally employing charcoal, modern methods use <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>, which has proven more economical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Processes_starting_from_bar_iron">Processes starting from bar iron</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Processes starting from bar iron">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blister_steel" title="Blister steel">Blister steel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crucible_steel" title="Crucible steel">Crucible steel</a></div>
<p>In these processes pig iron was refined (fined) in a <a href="/wiki/Finery_forge" title="Finery forge">finery forge</a> to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bar_iron" title="Bar iron">bar iron</a>, which was then used in steel-making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tyle_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-tyle-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The production of steel by the <a href="/wiki/Cementation_process" title="Cementation process">cementation process</a> was described in a treatise published in Prague in 1574 and was in use in <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a> from 1601. A similar process for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Case_hardening" title="Case hardening">case hardening</a> armor and files was described in a book published in <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a> in 1589. The process was introduced to England in about 1614 and used to produce such steel by Sir <a href="/wiki/Basil_Brooke_(metallurgist)" title="Basil Brooke (metallurgist)">Basil Brooke</a> at <a href="/wiki/Coalbrookdale" title="Coalbrookdale">Coalbrookdale</a> during the 1610s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarraclough198448–52_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarraclough198448–52-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The raw material for this process were bars of iron. During the 17th century, it was realized that the best steel came from <a href="/wiki/Oregrounds_iron" title="Oregrounds iron">oregrounds iron</a> of a region north of <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, Sweden. This was still the usual raw material source in the 19th century, almost as long as the process was used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannicaironandsteel_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannicaironandsteel-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Crucible steel is steel that has been melted in a <a href="/wiki/Crucible" title="Crucible">crucible</a> rather than having been <a href="/wiki/Forging" title="Forging">forged</a>, with the result that it is more homogeneous. Most previous furnaces could not reach high enough temperatures to melt the steel. The early modern crucible steel industry resulted from the invention of <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Huntsman" title="Benjamin Huntsman">Benjamin Huntsman</a> in the 1740s. Blister steel (made as above) was melted in a crucible or in a furnace, and cast (usually) into ingots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannicaironandsteel_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannicaironandsteel-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Processes_starting_from_pig_iron">Processes starting from pig iron</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Processes starting from pig iron">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siemens_Martin_Ofen_Brandenburg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3040" data-file-width="4048" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Siemens_Martin_Ofen_Brandenburg.jpg/240px-Siemens_Martin_Ofen_Brandenburg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Siemens_Martin_Ofen_Brandenburg.jpg/360px-Siemens_Martin_Ofen_Brandenburg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Siemens_Martin_Ofen_Brandenburg.jpg/480px-Siemens_Martin_Ofen_Brandenburg.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siemens_Martin_Ofen_Brandenburg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Siemens-Martin <a href="/wiki/Open_hearth_furnace" title="Open hearth furnace">open hearth furnace</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Brandenburg_an_der_Havel" title="Brandenburg an der Havel">Brandenburg</a> Museum of Industry</div></div></div>
<p>The modern era in <a href="/wiki/Steelmaking" title="Steelmaking">steelmaking</a> began with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bessemer" title="Henry Bessemer">Henry Bessemer</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Bessemer_process" title="Bessemer process">process</a> in 1855, the raw material for which was pig iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> His method let him produce steel in large quantities cheaply, thus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mild_steel" title="Mild steel">mild steel</a> came to be used for most purposes for which wrought iron was formerly used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The Gilchrist-Thomas process (or <i>basic Bessemer process</i>) was an improvement to the Bessemer process, made by lining the converter with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_(chemistry)" title="Basic (chemistry)">basic</a> material to remove phosphorus.
</p><p>Another 19th-century steelmaking process was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siemens-Martin_process" title="Siemens-Martin process">Siemens-Martin process</a>, which complemented the Bessemer process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannicaironandsteel_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-britannicaironandsteel-54">[54]</a></sup> It consisted of co-melting bar iron (or steel scrap) with pig iron.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allegheny_Ludlum_steel_furnace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2486" data-file-width="3209" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Allegheny_Ludlum_steel_furnace.jpg/240px-Allegheny_Ludlum_steel_furnace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Allegheny_Ludlum_steel_furnace.jpg/360px-Allegheny_Ludlum_steel_furnace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Allegheny_Ludlum_steel_furnace.jpg/480px-Allegheny_Ludlum_steel_furnace.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allegheny_Ludlum_steel_furnace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White-hot steel pouring out of an electric arc furnace.</div></div></div>
<p>These methods of steel production were rendered obsolete by the Linz-Donawitz process of <a href="/wiki/Basic_oxygen_steelmaking" title="Basic oxygen steelmaking">basic oxygen steelmaking</a> (BOS), developed in 1952,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zs_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-zs-58">[58]</a></sup> and other oxygen steel making methods. Basic oxygen steelmaking is superior to previous steelmaking methods because the oxygen pumped into the furnace limited impurities, primarily nitrogen, that previously had entered from the air used,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> and because, with respect to the open hearth process, the same quantity of steel from a BOS process is manufactured in one-twelfth the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zs_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-zs-58">[58]</a></sup> Today, <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc_furnace" title="Electric arc furnace">electric arc furnaces</a> (EAF) are a common method of reprocessing <a href="/wiki/Scrap" title="Scrap">scrap metal</a> to create new steel. They can also be used for converting pig iron to steel, but they use a lot of electrical energy (about 440 kWh per metric ton), and are thus generally only economical when there is a plentiful supply of cheap electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFruehanWakelin199848–52_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFruehanWakelin199848–52-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_steel_industry_(1850%E2%80%931970)" title="History of the steel industry (1850–1970)">History of the steel industry (1850–1970)</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_steel_industry_(1970%E2%80%93present)" title="History of the steel industry (1970–present)">History of the steel industry (1970–present)</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_steel_industry_trends" title="Global steel industry trends">Global steel industry trends</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steel_production_by_country" title="Steel production by country">Steel production by country</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_steel_producers" title="List of steel producers">List of steel producers</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:512px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Steel_production_by_country_map.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="633" data-file-width="1445" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Steel_production_by_country_map.PNG/510px-Steel_production_by_country_map.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Steel_production_by_country_map.PNG/765px-Steel_production_by_country_map.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Steel_production_by_country_map.PNG/1020px-Steel_production_by_country_map.PNG 2x" width="510"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steel_production_by_country_map.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Steel production (in million tons) by country in 2007</div></div></div>
<p>The steel industry is often considered an indicator of economic progress, because of the critical role played by steel in infrastructural and overall <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In 1980, there were more than 500,000 U.S. steelworkers. By 2000, the number of steelworkers had fallen to 224,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boom_and_bust" title="Boom and bust">economic boom</a> in China and India caused a massive increase in the demand for steel. Between 2000 and 2005, world steel demand increased by 6%. Since 2000, several Indian<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and Chinese steel firms have risen to prominence,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (October 2016)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Tata_Steel" title="Tata Steel">Tata Steel</a> (which bought <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corus_Group" title="Corus Group">Corus Group</a> in 2007), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baosteel_Group" title="Baosteel Group">Baosteel Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shagang_Group" title="Shagang Group">Shagang Group</a>. As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, though, <a href="/wiki/ArcelorMittal" title="ArcelorMittal">ArcelorMittal</a> is the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_steel_producers" title="List of steel producers">largest steel producer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/British_Geological_Survey" title="British Geological Survey">British Geological Survey</a> stated China was the top steel producer with about one-third of the world share; Japan, Russia, and the US followed respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The large production capacity of steel results also in a significant amount of carbon dioxide emissions inherent related to the main production route. In 2021, it was estimated that around 7% of the global greenhouse gas emissions resulted from the steel industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Reduction of these emissions are expected to come from a shift in the main production route using cokes, more recycling of steel and the application of carbon capture and storage or carbon capture and utilization technology.
</p><p>In 2008, steel began <a href="/wiki/Commodity_market" title="Commodity market">trading as a commodity</a> on the <a href="/wiki/London_Metal_Exchange" title="London Metal Exchange">London Metal Exchange</a>. At the end of 2008, the steel industry faced a sharp downturn that led to many cut-backs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling">Recycling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Recycling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrous_metal_recycling" title="Ferrous metal recycling">Ferrous metal recycling</a></div>
<p>Steel is one of the world's most-recycled materials, with a recycling rate of over 60% globally;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encarta-recycling_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-encarta-recycling-3">[3]</a></sup> in the United States alone, over 82,000,000 metric tons (81,000,000 long tons; 90,000,000 short tons) were recycled in the year 2008, for an overall recycling rate of 83%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenton-2008_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-fenton-2008-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>As more steel is produced than is scrapped, the amount of recycled raw materials is about 40% of the total of steel produced - in 2016, 1,628,000,000 tonnes (1.602<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> long tons; 1.795<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> short tons) of crude steel was produced globally, with 630,000,000 tonnes (620,000,000 long tons; 690,000,000 short tons) recycled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary">Contemporary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Contemporary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bethlehem_Steel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1004" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Bethlehem_Steel.jpg/240px-Bethlehem_Steel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Bethlehem_Steel.jpg/360px-Bethlehem_Steel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Bethlehem_Steel.jpg/480px-Bethlehem_Steel.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bethlehem_Steel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bethlehem_Steel" title="Bethlehem Steel">Bethlehem Steel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem,_Pennsylvania" title="Bethlehem, Pennsylvania">Bethlehem, Pennsylvania</a> (picture) was one of the world's largest manufacturers of steel before its closure in 2003.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Steel_grades" title="Steel grades">Steel grades</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon">Carbon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Carbon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modern steels are made with varying combinations of alloy metals to fulfill many purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-materialsengineer_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-materialsengineer-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_steel" title="Carbon steel">Carbon steel</a>, composed simply of iron and carbon, accounts for 90% of steel production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EM2_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-EM2-5">[5]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_alloy_steel" title="Low alloy steel">Low alloy steel</a> is alloyed with other elements, usually <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>, manganese, chromium, or nickel, in amounts of up to 10% by weight to improve the hardenability of thick sections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EM2_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-EM2-5">[5]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HSLA_steel" title="HSLA steel">High strength low alloy steel</a> has small additions (usually &lt; 2% by weight) of other elements, typically 1.5% manganese, to provide additional strength for a modest price increase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corporate_Average_Fuel_Economy" title="Corporate Average Fuel Economy">Corporate Average Fuel Economy</a> (CAFE) regulations have given rise to a new variety of steel known as Advanced High Strength Steel (AHSS). This material is both strong and ductile so that vehicle structures can maintain their current safety levels while using less material. There are several commercially available grades of AHSS, such as <a href="/wiki/Dual-phase_steel" title="Dual-phase steel">dual-phase steel</a>, which is heat treated to contain both a ferritic and martensitic microstructure to produce a formable, high strength steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Transformation Induced Plasticity (TRIP) steel involves special alloying and heat treatments to stabilize amounts of <a href="/wiki/Austenite" title="Austenite">austenite</a> at room temperature in normally austenite-free low-alloy ferritic steels. By applying strain, the austenite undergoes a <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase transition</a> to martensite without the addition of heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Twinning Induced Plasticity (TWIP) steel uses a specific type of strain to increase the effectiveness of work hardening on the alloy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon Steels are often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot-dip_galvanizing" title="Hot-dip galvanizing">galvanized</a>, through hot-dip or electroplating in <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> for protection against rust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alloy">Alloy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Alloy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alcator_C-Mod_superstructure_forging_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5675" data-file-width="4186" decoding="async" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Alcator_C-Mod_superstructure_forging_1.jpg/240px-Alcator_C-Mod_superstructure_forging_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Alcator_C-Mod_superstructure_forging_1.jpg/360px-Alcator_C-Mod_superstructure_forging_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Alcator_C-Mod_superstructure_forging_1.jpg/480px-Alcator_C-Mod_superstructure_forging_1.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alcator_C-Mod_superstructure_forging_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Forging a structural member out of steel</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stainless_steels" title="Stainless steels">Stainless steels</a> contain a minimum of 11% chromium, often combined with nickel, to resist <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a>. Some stainless steels, such as the <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_iron" title="Allotropes of iron">ferritic</a> stainless steels are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic" title="Magnetic">magnetic</a>, while others, such as the <a href="/wiki/Austenite" title="Austenite">austenitic</a>, are nonmagnetic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Corrosion-resistant steels are abbreviated as CRES.
</p><p>Alloy steels are plain-carbon steels in which small amounts of alloying elements like chromium and vanadium have been added. Some more modern steels include <a href="/wiki/Tool_steel" title="Tool steel">tool steels</a>, which are alloyed with large amounts of tungsten and <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> or other elements to maximize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solution_hardening" title="Solution hardening">solution hardening</a>. This also allows the use of <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_hardening" title="Precipitation hardening">precipitation hardening</a> and improves the alloy's temperature resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EM2_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-EM2-5">[5]</a></sup> Tool steel is generally used in axes, drills, and other devices that need a sharp, long-lasting cutting edge. Other special-purpose alloys include <a href="/wiki/Weathering_steel" title="Weathering steel">weathering steels</a> such as Cor-ten, which weather by acquiring a stable, rusted surface, and so can be used un-painted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maraging_steel" title="Maraging steel">Maraging steel</a> is alloyed with nickel and other elements, but unlike most steel contains little carbon (0.01%). This creates a very strong but still <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malleability" title="Malleability">malleable</a> steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eglin_steel" title="Eglin steel">Eglin steel</a> uses a combination of over a dozen different elements in varying amounts to create a relatively low-cost steel for use in <a href="/wiki/Bunker_buster" title="Bunker buster">bunker buster</a> weapons. Hadfield steel (after Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hadfield" title="Robert Hadfield">Robert Hadfield</a>) or manganese steel contains 12–14% manganese which when abraded strain-hardens to form a very hard skin which resists wearing. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Continuous_track" title="Continuous track">tank tracks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulldozer#Blade" title="Bulldozer">bulldozer blade</a> edges, and cutting blades on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jaws_of_life" title="Jaws of life">jaws of life</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standards">Standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most of the more commonly used steel alloys are categorized into various grades by standards organizations. For example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_of_Automotive_Engineers" title="Society of Automotive Engineers">Society of Automotive Engineers</a> has a series of <a href="/wiki/SAE_steel_grades" title="SAE steel grades">grades</a> defining many types of steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bringas_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-bringas-79">[79]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/ASTM_International" title="ASTM International">American Society for Testing and Materials</a> has a separate set of standards, which define alloys such as <a href="/wiki/A36_steel" title="A36 steel">A36 steel</a>, the most commonly used structural steel in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Industrial_Standards" title="Japanese Industrial Standards">JIS</a> also defines a series of steel grades that are being used extensively in Japan as well as in developing countries.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Steel-wool.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1461" data-file-width="1847" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Steel-wool.jpg/240px-Steel-wool.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Steel-wool.jpg/360px-Steel-wool.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Steel-wool.jpg/480px-Steel-wool.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steel-wool.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A roll of steel wool</div></div></div>
<p>Iron and steel are used widely in the construction of roads, railways, other infrastructure, appliances, and buildings. Most large modern structures, such as <a href="/wiki/Stadium#The_modern_stadium" title="Stadium">stadiums</a> and skyscrapers, bridges, and airports, are supported by a steel skeleton. Even those with a concrete structure employ steel for reinforcing. It sees widespread use in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_appliances" title="Major appliances">major appliances</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">cars</a>. Despite the growth in usage of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, it is still the main material for car bodies. Steel is used in a variety of other construction materials, such as bolts, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nail_(engineering)" title="Nail (engineering)">nails</a> and <a href="/wiki/Screw" title="Screw">screws</a> and other household products and cooking utensils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other common applications include <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Offshore_construction" title="Offshore construction">offshore construction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aerospace" title="Aerospace">aerospace</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_goods" title="White goods">white goods</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Washing_machine" title="Washing machine">washing machines</a>), <a href="/wiki/Heavy_equipment" title="Heavy equipment">heavy equipment</a> such as bulldozers, office furniture, <a href="/wiki/Steel_wool" title="Steel wool">steel wool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tool" title="Tool">tool</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Armour" title="Armour">armour</a> in the form of personal vests or <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_armour" title="Vehicle armour">vehicle armour</a> (better known as <a href="/wiki/Rolled_homogeneous_armour" title="Rolled homogeneous armour">rolled homogeneous armour</a> in this role).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical">Historical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Historical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_steel_knife.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1119" data-file-width="1755" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Carbon_steel_knife.jpg/240px-Carbon_steel_knife.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Carbon_steel_knife.jpg/360px-Carbon_steel_knife.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Carbon_steel_knife.jpg/480px-Carbon_steel_knife.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_steel_knife.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A carbon steel knife</div></div></div>
<p>Before the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Bessemer_process" title="Bessemer process">Bessemer process</a> and other modern production techniques, steel was expensive and was only used where no cheaper alternative existed, particularly for the cutting edge of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knives" title="Knives">knives</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Razors" title="Razors">razors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sword" title="Sword">swords</a>, and other items where a hard, sharp edge was needed. It was also used for <a href="/wiki/Spring_(device)" title="Spring (device)">springs</a>, including those used in <a href="/wiki/Clock" title="Clock">clocks and watches</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannicaironandsteel_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-britannicaironandsteel-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the advent of speedier and thriftier production methods, steel has become easier to obtain and much cheaper. It has replaced wrought iron for a multitude of purposes. However, the availability of plastics in the latter part of the 20th century allowed these materials to replace steel in some applications due to their lower fabrication cost and weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber" title="Carbon fiber">Carbon fiber</a> is replacing steel in some cost insensitive applications such as sports equipment and high-end automobiles.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long">Long</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Long">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_viaduct_La_Polvorilla,_Salta_Argentina.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1832" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/The_viaduct_La_Polvorilla%2C_Salta_Argentina.jpg/240px-The_viaduct_La_Polvorilla%2C_Salta_Argentina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/The_viaduct_La_Polvorilla%2C_Salta_Argentina.jpg/360px-The_viaduct_La_Polvorilla%2C_Salta_Argentina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/The_viaduct_La_Polvorilla%2C_Salta_Argentina.jpg/480px-The_viaduct_La_Polvorilla%2C_Salta_Argentina.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_viaduct_La_Polvorilla,_Salta_Argentina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A steel bridge</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Steel_tower.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Steel_tower.jpg/240px-Steel_tower.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Steel_tower.jpg/360px-Steel_tower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Steel_tower.jpg/480px-Steel_tower.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steel_tower.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A steel pylon suspending <a href="/wiki/Overhead_power_line" title="Overhead power line">overhead power lines</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li>As reinforcing bars and mesh in <a href="/wiki/Reinforced_concrete" title="Reinforced concrete">reinforced concrete</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railroad_tracks" title="Railroad tracks">Railroad tracks</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Structural_steel" title="Structural steel">Structural steel</a> in modern buildings and bridges</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wire" title="Wire">Wires</a></li>
<li>Input to reforging applications</li></ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stainless">Stainless</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Stainless">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sauce_boat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2036" data-file-width="3192" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Sauce_boat.jpg/240px-Sauce_boat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Sauce_boat.jpg/360px-Sauce_boat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Sauce_boat.jpg/480px-Sauce_boat.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sauce_boat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A stainless steel <a href="/wiki/Sauce_boat" title="Sauce boat">gravy boat</a></div></div></div>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 20em;">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-background">Low-background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Low-background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Low-background_steel" title="Low-background steel">Low-background steel</a></div>
<p>Steel manufactured after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> became <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">contaminated</a> with <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclides</a> by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_testing" title="Nuclear weapons testing">nuclear weapons testing</a>. Low-background steel, steel manufactured prior to 1945, is used for certain radiation-sensitive applications such as <a href="/wiki/Geiger_counter" title="Geiger counter">Geiger counters</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation_shielding" title="Radiation shielding">radiation shielding</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Steel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>Mark Reutter, <i><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=bdkUfDoY24QC" rel="nofollow">Making Steel: Sparrows Point and the Rise and Ruin of American Industrial Might</a></i>. University of Illinois Press, 2005.</li>
<li>Duncan Burn, <i><a class="external text" href="http://questia.com/PM.qst?a=o&amp;d=3914930" rel="nofollow">The Economic History of Steelmaking, 1867–1939: A Study in Competition</a></i>. Cambridge University Press, 1961.</li>
<li>Harukiyu Hasegawa, <i><a class="external text" href="http://questia.com/PM.qst?a=o&amp;d=108742046" rel="nofollow">The Steel Industry in Japan: A Comparison with Britain</a></i>. Routledge, 1996.</li>
<li>J.C. Carr and W. Taplin, <i><a class="external text" href="http://questia.com/PM.qst?a=o&amp;d=808791" rel="nofollow">History of the British Steel Industry</a></i>. Harvard University Press, 1962.</li>
<li>H. Lee Scamehorn, <i><a class="external text" href="http://questia.com/PM.qst?a=o&amp;d=94821694" rel="nofollow">Mill &amp; Mine: The Cf&amp;I in the Twentieth Century</a></i>. University of Nebraska Press, 1992.</li>
<li>Warren, Kenneth, <i><a class="external text" href="http://eh.net/bookreviews/library/0558" rel="nofollow">Big Steel: The First Century of the United States Steel Corporation, 1901–2001</a></i>. University of Pittsburgh Press, 2001.</li></ul>
